His hand-me-downs and lack of assertiveness make him the subject of endless ridicule. His only outlet is drawing, which affords him praise everywhere but at home. He finds an unlikely ally in Leslie his new next-door neighbor.Leslie is a tomboy. She declares her presence by beating all the boys in a foot race. This doesn't exactly gather her any votes for "Miss Popular", especially with Janice the school bully. But there's something special about her.Leslie's parents are fiction authors, which Jess declares to be "making things up". But Leslie sees that as creative, which is her escape. After an invitation from Leslie to "keep your eyes closed and your mind open", the pair creates a domain that only they rule, which they christen Terabithia.Within the confines of Terabithia, anything and everything is possible. The kids develop an opportunity to exercise their underlying insecurities by reacting to their hypothetical surroundings. This paves the way for a set of special effects that would make Peter Jackson proud (especially since his company created them!) Even the best of utopias, though, eventually contain a reality check, not unlike the passing from childhood to adulthood. The tragic turn of events should bring a tear to even the most hardened cynic. It hits like a brick wall, enhanced by the strong performances of the entire cast.Be careful of the marketing, though. While being a family-oriented film, it isn't really a film for youngsters. Children under the age of seven or so may not understand the film or could be frightened by some of the imagery. Pre-teens or teenagers could be sent to see this one without supervision.But if families choose to see it together, it could start a dialogue ? something sadly lacking these days. Parents could even share Katherine Paterson's novel before or after the film. But the main point is that an imagination can be even more important than the latest game system or the hottest new toy (What a novel idea!) "Bridge to Terabithia", unlike many other films I watch, affected me personally. Every single person, regardless of age, monetary worth, location, or anything else, needs a creative outlet of some kind in order to flourish.Thanks for allowing me to indulge mine.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A fantastical fantasy</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Bridge to Terabithia/2007/***Main Stars: Josh Hutcherson AnnaSophia Robb Zooey Deschanel Robert PatrickI read the book Bridge to Terabithia by Katherine Paterson in fifth grade. When I first heard that Bridge to Terabithia was going to be a movie in 2007, I was in fifth grade. I was fifth grade in the years 2003 and 2004. I am now in eighth grade in the year 2007, and the movie has just come out. I took them three years to make the movie? Perhaps they had just started the script when I was in fifth grade. But three years to make a movie still amazes me. The script is by David Patterson and co-written by Jeff Stockwell. David Paterson is the son of Katherine Paterson, the author of the book. You can tell. The film is directed by Gabor Csupo (pronounced "Chew-po"), who was a big hit with creating the Nickelodeon series, "Rugrats", which I loved when I was a little kid and which (I admit it) I still like now (the first two movies now; not the show). The first two movies were clever and movie buffs would recognize a lot of scenes that somewhat satire famous films, like the introduction in Rugrats in Paris satires The Godfather, which was an excellent scene. The third "Rugrats" film was shrill and annoying. I decided to see Bridge to Terabithia for those two reasons: I read it in fifth grade, and Gabor Csupo's accomplishments impressed me. It turns out I liked it. If you saw the previews for the film, then you might think the film is filled with special effects. It's really not. The special effects are the most minor things in this film. The film concentrates more on the characters' creativity and imagination, which is a good thing. I truly would rather see a movie that shows more what humans are like and how they act rather than see a brainless adventure film, where the only adventure is seeing overwhelming special effects pop before your eyes. I seemed to have overrated Roland Emmerich's The Day After Tomorrow by a tad. That concentrated more on special effects than anything. However, I must add that it did have good acting. Then again, when I reviewed the film, I was an immature child. I've grown a little since the beginning of 2005. The main characters, Jesse Aarons (Josh Hutcherson) and Leslie Burke (AnnaSophia Robb), are the two that have similar, creative imaginations. They both get picked on at school and both daydream. As for Leslie, she seems a little more of the adventurous type than Jesse. However, Jesse seems to learn from her to be able to imagine the world of Terabithia. Jesse's father, Jack (Robert Patrick), is the type of father that is non-imaginative and a little rough around the edges. The mother, Nancy (Katrina Cerio), is the type of mother that is a worrier but she doesn't have as big of a part as Jack does. Jesse realizes his dad doesn't seem to understand his creative side, which we, as the audience, can realize too. Leslie's parents are completely different: they are full of happiness and adventure. They are both authors. That may show why Leslie is so creative. Then again, Jesse is creative and neither of his parents seems to have that disposition. So what does that show? It shows that Jesse is unique. And it shows that Leslie is also unique no matter how much she may resemble her parents. It still shows that everyone is slightly different from other people. That was one of the main themes I picked up from the film. Jesse has a crush on the music teacher, Ms. Edmonds (Zooey Deschanel), at his school, who recognizes his talent by taking a peek at his drawings. It just so happens she even takes him to a museum to look at more art painted by famous artists/painters. There is a bully at school who picks on Leslie a lot. Does she learn her mistake and move on no matter what happens at home? This is another main theme in the film. The bully also happens to show up as a different creature in the film which shows her new self. Jesse may think his life is bad but when we see it, his life is excellent and filled with different adventures all the way through. A tragic event happens in the film which lowers his happiness. But as for his imagination, it still blooms on as life goes on. And soon, he would gain joviality as his imagination blooms and he creates a whole new world inside his head. Movie directed by Gabor Csupo.</fullReview>

		<date>2007-11-10</date>
		<username>Howard Schumann</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Never feels authentic</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Katherine Paterson's novel "Bridge to Terabithia" is the moving story of two lonely children who rely on each other's strength to overcome their own shortcomings. It is a highly symbolic and multi-layered coming of age story that deals with family, friendship, imagination, and death. Sadly in the Walden Media/Disney version of Bridge to Terabithia, the combination of phony uplifting messages, wooden performances, and unimpressive special effects contribute to a film experience that offers little joy or believability, even for the 8 to 12 year old age group it is geared for. While it is decent family entertainment and its theme about the power of imagination is most welcome, the fact that so many people call this film a masterpiece only indicates to me the extent that we have lost the ability in our society to distinguish between art and kitsch.For those unfamiliar with Walden Media, it is a film production and publishing company owned by Philip Anschutz, a billionaire oil tycoon, recently identified by Beliefnet as the tenth most powerful Christian in Hollywood. Walden's stated mission is to promote so-called family values - in other words to counter the left-wingers by bringing conservative Christian values back to Hollywood, one film at a time. In Bridges, the Christian angle (also present in the book) is introduced in a visit to church and a discussion between 10 and 11-year olds about whether believing in Jesus will keep you from going to hell. The scene is an obvious intrusion that seems out of place in the context of the film.Filmed in New Zealand and set in an unnamed rural area, Jesse Aarons (Josh Hutcherson) is a fifth grader who is bullied by his classmates presumably because he is poor and likes to draw. In the wonderful world of Disney, bullying consists of stealing twinkies and charging money to go to the bathroom (heck, I had to pay protection money just to stay alive). Jesse never responds even though his athletic build indicates that he would more than hold his own in a fight. His home life offers no solace either.His parents heap a lot of attention on his four sisters and either ignore Jesse or constantly yell at him. Only his music teacher (Zooey Deschanel) in the rural school recognizes his talent and takes him to an art museum. Why the teachers do not caution the bullies or try to stop their actions is unclear. Attempting to show off his running skills, Jesse enters a racing competition but is beaten by Leslie Burke (AnnaSophia Robb), a new girl in his class who has moved next door to him. In spite of her good looks and bubbly personality, Leslie's tomboyishness and ability as a writer keep her on the outside looking in and the two form a tentative friendship based on mutual need.Leslie's parents are free-spirited writers who are absorbed in their work, leaving Leslie and Jesse to spend their after school hours in imaginative play where they create a world called Terabithia in the forest that surrounds their home. In Terabithia, trees become giants, squirrels turn into beasts, and Jesse and Leslie gain magical powers. Of course, they fight actual battles with attackers so the line between what is real and what is imagined is blurred and the film can straddle both worlds. Through his interaction with Leslie, however, Jesse begins to come out of his shell and develop some self confidence but a tragic turn compels him to use all of his new found strength just to stay afloat.The heart of the film of course is the friendship between the two pre-teens and its success depends on whether or not you believe that they are troubled outcasts eager to escape from the harsh reality of their world. Robb, however, with her perpetual smile and high energy never seems real in the role of a social misfit and Hutcherson has only a few expressions, most of which are variations of sullen and the two leads lack any discernible chemistry together. In spite of a few genuinely touching moments, Bridge to Terabithia never feels authentic - either as a fantasy or as a tale of friendship and loss.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Discover a place that will never leave you, and a friendship that will change you forever</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Jesse "Jess" Aarons comes from a poor farmer family with four annoying sisters, he finds no haven at school where he's teased by everyone. The only thing Jess has is running; after training all summer for the big race on the school athletic day, he and every other boy racing are beat by new girl Leslie Burke. Like Jess, Leslie is teased by her fellow students, but unlike Jess she doesn't let it bother her. After a rocky start, they find common ground and together create the imaginary world of Terabithia as a haven from their struggles at school and at home. Leslie teaches Jess to open his mind to every possibility and leave reality behind.Must I say this is the best film of the year, so far. It is really a heart-breaking film seen through the eyes of teenagers, which is done so authentically and seamlessly. The trailer isn't so, showing only the effects throughout the whole film. No, this is not an effects movie. This is a movie about friendship and how to overcome the harsh reality of this world.For parents out there, you should consider the PG rating because young kids will be heartbroken to find what will happen in the movie. And for the parents, this is not a kid's film but a film for the entire family to watch and learn what it has to say.And to talk about puberty between these two once young actors. The performances are great, here. Josh Hutcherson and Anna Sophia Robb both have great connection with each other as friends but I will admit one small thing bothered me about the film, although I'm not going to spoil it here.Terabithia doesn't focus on effects and action. It is driven by characters, the story, and the heart of this beautiful tale. Some who see this may be heartbroken but I think it's worth the watch if you really want to see something very great.</fullReview>

		<date>2007-09-16</date>
		<username>Brett Chandler (Thunderbuck)</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>"Unexpected" in all the right ways</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>As BRIDGE TO TERABITHIA begins, it looks pretty familiar. Bullied kid, overlooked in his family, with a stern, seemingly-uncaring father, about to see his life changed by the new girl in class...And, of course, we know that the bullies are going to receive a comeuppance, the dad will break down and show that he really DOES have a heart, and that the boy and his new friend will find some magical world through the power of imagination.Except here, it doesn't happen exactly the way we've come to expect, and I'm pleased to say that this is as original and mature a script I've seen for a supposed "family" film in some time, from the clever animation in the title sequence to its moving ending.The first clue that this is something a little better than the standard family fare is that the performances and the script both ring true; Josh Hutcherson's character of Jess is totally believable, seemingly lost in the shuffle both at home and at school. The only thing that makes him special, being the fastest runner in school, is taken away from him when he's beaten in a footrace by new girl Leslie (AnnaSophia Robb).Jess and Leslie soon find they share an important bond; they are both very creative. Jess is constantly sketching and illustrating, and Leslie writes marvellously. In an early sequence, Leslie reads a paragraph on scuba diving that is so evocative that we see the bubbles rising from her mouth. Neighbors, Jess and Leslie are soon spending time together after school in the forest, where they begin to construct their own fantastic land of creatures.The first thing I REALLY liked about this movie is where the writers drew the line between reality and Jess and Leslie's fantasy; this is something they do to have fun, and entertain themselves. Yes, they gain some strength and confidence from their friendship, but their fantasies don't solve all their problems for them, either. The CGI sequences were used to sell this film as a family-friendly fantasy, but in truth they could have made this movie completely without them.A major character dies in the course of the movie, and I was concerned that the wonderful, believable balance established up to that point would be sacrificed. In fact, this is where the movie really begins to shine.Yes, the bullies are dealt with, in part through the confidence that Jess and Leslie find through their friendship. Yes, the stern dad (in a great turn from Robert Patrick) shows his heart, but in a scene so understated and quick it would be easy to miss if it weren't so convincing and moving. Did the co-producer of the RUGRATS cartoon series really direct this? Wow.To call BRIDGE TO TERABITHIA a great family movie might be accurate, but damns it with faint praise; it's a great MOVIE, period. Like TO KILL A MOCKINGBIRD or IT'S A WONDERFUL LIFE, this movie transcends the "family" label to become worthwhile for nearly any audience. And, no, I have no problem putting BRIDGE TO TERABITHIA in that company.</fullReview>
	</review>

The boy has an additional problem in being treated badly by his poor parents. They think up a country (Terabithia) and fantasize about the inhabitants. This is where the few fantasy moments occur. They learn lessons from their fantasy play which they apply successfully in the real school world.The movie itself is quite touching and the actors play well. I could recommend this movie to anyone who likes a children's' drama movie, but especially Not to lover of "pure" fantasy movies. I still hope the IMDb staff will remove the fantasy denomination.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Better then I thought</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Bridge to Terabithia is not a bad movie. In fact I would put it in the same category as Narnia. Some things worked for me, and some things did not, but overall in the end It shows something very special. Bridge to Terabithia is set and revolves around a young lonely boy, meeting a young lonely girl, they are both social outcasts at their school and after a connection is struck they soon realise that they can escape their lives by using their imaginations. Sure enough like the title suggests, they create a mythical land known as "Terabithia" in the Forest nearby. They venture into the forest every afternoon, fighting fantasy creatures, building their fort, and ultimately talking and learning from one another. The numerous sub-plots revolve around the school they go too, and by using generic bully's and characters the director has made the whole thing more accessible to a younger audience.For me I found that this part of the movie, was more interesting then the actual storyline. I don't really know why, but I found myself being disappointed with the happenings and occurrences within Terabithia. It wasn't very exciting, whereas the stuff at school was. However being one of the few people who have not read the books I found the ending very surprising, and to be honest quite thought provoking. When I found out that the girl had drowned, attempting to get into Terabithia it had a profound impact on me. I was shocked, and this emotion made the next few scenes all the more effective and powerful. I was sad that she had died, but still I did not want her to come back. After the boy finally gets over her death, and the almost bitter sweet ending is before you, I suddenly realized that the movie was actually not that bad. I had gotten over the boring writing, and almost lazy storyline by the shock ending and sad emotions the final scenes had delivered.Overall Bridge to Terabithia shows something special, and thought provoking in the end, and that is what I'll remember about it.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Most detractors of this film seem to focus on how they've been deceived by the promotional material, which lead them to believe they were going to see a re-hash of Land of Narnia (or something like that).Critising something simply because 'it wasn't what was expected' simply exposes the prejudices of the observer, and indicates that the reviewer's objectivity is compromised.Where this film scores highly is that it portrays children's worlds in a very immediate and powerful way without being mawkish or sentimental. It deals with real issues, such as bereavement, bullying and family life very directly. More importantly, however, it goes on to show how these issues and their associated difficulties can be transcended, by reaching for higher causes and considering the welfare of others.The other great thing about this film is that it encourages consideration of art, imagination, and philosophy. It ponders the nature of life and divinity in a simple, inquisitive and ultimately positive way. Furthermore, it deprecates the practise of excessive TV viewing, which is to be applauded.The performances reflect pure emotions not only because children's emotions are generally purer than adults, but also many of the actors do an outstanding job.I heartily recommend this film to all children between the ages of 6-106. 		<shortDescription>Hey fellow IMDb reviewers: Get a life!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>99% of these comments fall into either of these two categories:1. OMG, NOT FOR KIDS! Somebody dies! Attack on Christianity! Not like the trailer! 2. This is the best movie ever!In reality it is somewhere in between. To the haters, realize this movie is really about young adolescence NOT childhood. They two are very different stages in life. What boy of 12 or 13 hasn't had a killer crush on some 20-something woman? I know I did. What boy of 12 or 13 hasn't felt the weight of adult responsibility starting to tug on him and escaped through fantasy? I know I did. What group of children HASN'T had the discussion, away from parents, about the fear of eternal damnation? You are lying if you say you didn't. Remember when girls were tall, and seemed to be full of confidence, while we were still short and lacking it? I do. All these things are fairly normal parts of early adolescence, as are whisperings of parental abuse, and confronting issues of death.All the above does NOT mean that this movie had some agenda to push on children at the expense of their parents. If anything you should be thankful for being reminded of what being that age is about... maybe it will help you talk to your kids. Two of my friends died when I was a teenager... One of my son's schoolmates was killed by a falling tree a few years back. Death is part of life and we can't shelter children from that fact. This movie provided a good prompting for that issue and others. On the other hand, this is not a cinematic masterpiece either. It is just a nice little movie. I brought my 13 year old son, and we both enjoyed it as a way to pass a Sunday afternoon. It provided some nice issues for further discussion, but neither of us are gushing about it. I gave it a 6 because it fell right in the middle - not really a great movie, but certainly not awful. I added one star for the fact that after reading the rantings of so many of the folks that hated it, I figured they needed the poke in the eye more than the folks that loved this movie. --chuck in the rural pacific northwest</fullReview>

That in its self put me in a more critical frame of mind for this movie. Sorry. I think kids get way too much candy these days. It is just not reality.However, the story has it's moment. I don't think you could pick a more photogenic pair of kids than AnnaSophia Robb or Josh Hutcherson, and they were well paired for this movie. I seemed that peripheral story of the Aaron family had a back seat, as did the stories involving school. The primary focus was on a young girl (AnnaSophias' Leslie) who had her life downgraded because of parental decision to move, and the life of the boy she lived next door to (Josh Hutchersons' Jesse). Through various situations, Jesse befriends Leslie who eventually unlocks his inspirational sources and sets his artistic mind free.The use of CGI is somewhat limited, which in a way is kinda refreshing. It allows for more character development (even tho it seemed a tad weak). Graphically beautiful and shot in lush wooded country, there was plenty of room for the imagination of a child to be liberated. The closest thing to a spoiler I'm going to get to is that disaster strikes the heart of this boy 7/8ths of the movie, which was another point I needed to make: I should have felt more impact, but it just seems to pass by... and even though this disaster culminates in honor, the honor is a little sideways than dead center. That is only my opinion, as is all other reviews I have done here.The Bridge To Terabithia is a worthy watch. Not quite full price, but definitely a big screen event during a matinée. It has charm. It has some charisma. Most importantly, it has imagination, and a simple story that is fairly easy to follow: Rural boy meets new neighbor girl, forms a relationship that inspires him to overcome his reticence about pursuing his imagination and artistic abilities. For kids, this is a good thumbs up, but for adults, I remain ... -Astralan</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>So many positives to rave about, yet we are talking about marketing techniques!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Apparently there is some marketing scandal, where a movie's trailers have somewhat misrepresented a film. I am certain that, as this is the first time in history that that has ever happened, some heads will roll. Not. Not one newspaper or television review that I saw before I went to this film stated that it was anything different than it was. As an adult I don't even choose to see a film based entirely on the trailer alone, let alone let my kids see one based on the trailer. I always just assumed that the rating PG was advice to not let 4, 5 and 6 year olds go and see it. I didn't let my five year old watch Narnia either. I'm not certain animal sacrifice and kindergarten are a great mix.Sadly, all of this negativity seems to be missing the fact that this is an honest and heartfelt recreation of Katherine Patterson's novel. It is particularly good for the 10 - 14 crowd, which is where the novel ends up commonly used in school. Like others, I was worried that this was going to be a Disney disaster just like Where the Red Fern Grows, or Paramount's recent live action Charlotte's Web. They deal with similar themes to this one, but for some reason neither movie appealed to me, even though I love both books.This time around, I was pleasantly surprised. The two main actors solidly interact and we care about them enough to make the end of the story have an impact. The story is well told, the visual effects add a sense of whimsy, although it is a little ironic that a story about the power of your imagination has to spell out the power of imagination. The idea that people need more "realistic" fantastic imaginations from the minds of 11 year olds will just start me on another rant, so I won't go there.Just like the book, there are many interesting entry points into discussion with kids about a wide variety of topics. The book's most controversial moments are pretty much kept intact; the discussion about the literal interpretation of the concept of damnation as it applies to the bible, children and loss being the biggest and most obvious. Then there is guilt and grief. There is the difference between the crush that Jesse has for his teacher and Leslie's father's statement that Leslie "loved" Jesse. There is the rich nature of the title, is it literally related to the end of the movie or is there a metaphorical meaning too. And there are so many more! In other words, it is fairly intelligent and offers many ways to actually talk about the experience of watching the movie.How horrible.The only knock that I have on the movie is that some of the things seem weird (a teacher taking a student on a weekend field trip, the music program consisting exclusively of singing folk songs to guitar, etc) partly because the time period of the "1970's" is not explicitly stated anywhere. It is less jarring when you are not trying to put it into a 2007 context. To parents that are planning on seeing this film with their families, make sure you leave time to hit the food courts for fruit juice and talk time after this movie. This movie has meaning to it and requires more work than some of the other twaddle that is out there. If you are not a parent, keep in mind that it is a children's film and I imagine you will still find yourself moved to tears by the story and remembering your own awkwardness while growing up.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Accurately follows the book</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I read the book in 6th grade. I didn't remember many of the details but knew what the story was about. I didn't have a copy of the book to refresh my memory with, so I read the wikipedia article about it. This article provides a good summary of what both the book and movie are about. I wanted to see this movie for personal reasons. I had a friend in high school who was killed in a tragic car accident. It took me a long, long time to get over it. I was very impressed with the acting of the various characters. Both Josh Hutcherson and AnnaSophia Robb do very well with their leading roles, despite limited experience on the big screen. Robert Patrick was great as the strict but loving father, even though his previous screen appearance as a sociopathic jewelry thief in John Cena's not so epic "The Marine" was anything but stellar. I also liked Lauren Clinton's portrayal of the school bully Janice Avery. Her performance was very dynamic despite playing a minor character in the story. The only difference I can find between the book and the movie is that the book makes reference to Washington, DC as the author spent part of her life in Takoma Park, MD, whereas the movie takes place in a generic rural location and makes no mention of Washington, DC. This is minor and does not detract from the movie. Contrary to the trailer, this movie is not another "Chronicles of Narnia" or "Harry Potter" magic tale. It is about the struggles of growing up, learning to use imagination and courage, and being able to cope with loss. The special effects seem sparse at times, but they are not necessary for the movie's theme. Sure, Disney could have spend another 30 million dollars to add 30 more minutes of fantasy special effects, but the movie still would have ended the same way. Without a doubt, see this movie. It has very a powerful and moving message to it.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Wonderful family fare that captures emotion better than many films do these days.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Boy, what a wonderful experience I just had.. It's not very common for great films to come out in the first couple months of the year, but Disney's latest is one of the most wonderful family films I've seen in quite some time.If there is any way I could briefly describe this film, I'm very surprised to find myself saying that it's basically a family-friendly version of "Pan's Labyrinth." In "Pan's," Ofelia is a girl who escapes the troubles she faces in the real world by traveling into the woods and letting the magical world she discovers take her over. In this film, Jesse and Leslie escape the problems they face both at school and at home by traveling into the woods to a magical world where they are capable of anything.Like with "Pan's Labyrinth," the viewer will probably be surprised by how the majority of the film takes place in the real world instead of in the fantasy world that you expected to see lots of. However, the scenes that involve reality truly are the most magical parts. Josh Hutcherson and Annasophia Robb may not get Oscar recognition in the near future, but their two personalities flood on screen and are very impressive for kids their age. They give very relatable performances as kids who want to know that there is more to the world than what they are forced to stare eye and eye with every day.The rest of the cast is wonderful as well, including some very nice performances from the other school kids (although the school scenes sometimes get too unrealistic as some things that happen at that school just don't happen in real life), Zooey Deschanel as the music teacher of Jesse and Leslie (you may remember her as the love interest of Will Ferrell in "Elf"), as well as the four parents of these two children. Nothing here is close to extraordinary when you compare it to some of the performances that are competing at the Oscars this year, but for a family film they are definitely all the film needs.This film is not as good as "Pan's Labyrinth," as that film really touched me more and had a more realistic touch when you consider the very unrealistic and fantasy plot, not to mention some of the fantastic performances from the cast, but this truly is better than many of the family movies out today (I can say without hesitation that this is much better fantasy family fare than the likes of recent films such as "Eragon" and "Happily N'Ever After," two films that exploit action and comedy, respectively, to entertain the viewer). This film touches base with real emotions and has some moments that will at least make you teary-eyed. For a family film, it has some very serious plot material.There may be enjoyable family films that come out later in this year, such as "Shrek the Third" and "Harry Potter and the Order of the Phoenix," and I know that I'll definitely be seeing both of those movies. But if you want to see a film that truly moves you in the way that movies rarely do, "Bridge to Terabithia" is a film that you should not miss.A-</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Rob_Taylor</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>How to shatter childhood dreams....with a really BIG rock!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>For the majority of this movie, I was reasonably well entertained and it has a simple charm that automatically endears it to the viewer, no matter what their age.But be warned, gentle reader, that this is all part of the wind-up to one of the most jarring and (in my opinion) unnecessarily abrupt changes of mood that will leave you scowling at the screen and wondering just what kind of movies are considered "kid safe" these days.For those that have small children I'd seriously advise avoiding this movie. The very, very young will likely not catch on to what has happened, but parents should expect a raft of awkward questions from anyone in the 5-9 age group about life, death and what is going on.To summarise, after making a new friend (his only real friend), Jesse, the main character, is abruptly told that said friend has died. To add insult to injury at this point, the likely circumstances of the death are then spelt out to him, scene by scene, in some bizarre act of cruelty.Although such sudden and pointless deaths are a facet of our real lives, to see this played out in a film which is essentially a childhood fantasy is quite awful. Sure, life is hard, but do we really want our kids to learn this fact from a movie? To be brief, after this scene, the whole movie turned rather sour. In another head scratching move, all of the lead character Jesse's other problems appear to resolve themselves after the death in some weird "every cloud has a silver lining" kind of effect.Additionally, if I was a kid, with a very real seeming fantasy world I could visit, and my best friend had just died, I'm pretty sure I'd imagine that friend still alive in the fantasy world. But not in this movie. It's like Jesse's imagination has some freakish block - I can imagine things that never existed, but not someone I was very close to?In short, don't take sensitive kids to see this film. Used to be, Disney films were a safe bet for parents. These days....not so much. It's a good, charming film ruined by dragging reality into it. Unsatisfying would be the politest term I would use to describe this movie.</fullReview>

	<review>
		<date>2007-05-24</date>
		<username>James Hitchcock</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Sombre and Depressing</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Like a number of other viewers, I had got the impression from the rather misleading publicity material that this film was a fantasy along the lines of "The Chronicles of Narnia", a film which I rather enjoyed even though I am not a follower of C. S. Lewis's brand of evangelical fundamentalism. Of course, "Bridge to Terabithia" is not a fantasy of that nature, but it does have some similarities to "The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe". (Even the name "Terabithia" was derived by Katherine Patterson, author of the original novel, from "Terebinthia", an island in Lewis's Narnia books). Both concern children who find their way into a magical kingdom, populated by all sorts of strange and magical creatures, some good, some evil. In "The Lion?.." the entrance to the alternative universe is through the back of a wardrobe; in "Terabithia" it can be reached by swinging on a rope across a stream. The difference is that in Lewis's books we are supposed to accept that the Pevensie children actually do enter the land of Narnia, whereas the kingdom of Terabithia has no existence outside the children's imagination.The main characters are a boy called Jess and a girl called Leslie. (To British eyes that looks the wrong way round. Over here Leslie is normally a boy's name, the equivalent girl's name being spelled "Lesley", and Jess is most commonly a diminutive of Jessica). Both are outsiders and the victims of the bullies at their school, of which there are a surprising number. Rudeness, sarcasm and snobbery seem to be core subjects on the American school curriculum. Jess is bullied because of his family's poverty (his father is a struggling farmer) and his imaginative, sensitive nature. It seems strange that the attractive, outgoing Leslie should be unpopular, but she is taunted because she is a new girl in the school and because her parents are seen as eccentric. (They do not, for example, own a television). Although Jess and Leslie are in their early teens, rather younger than the age at which most children start to take a romantic interest in the opposite sex, they become best friends, relying on one another for mutual support against the bullies. The Kingdom of Terabithia, in which they fight battles against the Dark Master on behalf of the oppressed inhabitants, is their fantasy world into which they can escape. Jess also sees it as a way of escaping from his troubled home life and his parents, especially his father, a strict disciplinarian who has little sympathy with Jess's imaginative flights of fancy.For much of its length, the film is a fairly normal coming-of-age story, but abruptly changes tone about two-thirds of the way through when Leslie, attempting to cross the stream without Jess, falls into the water and drowns. To kill off a major character as sweet and lovable as young Leslie is always a risk for any film to take, and "Bridge to Terabithia" never recovers from this point on, especially as young Anna Sophia Robb, who plays Leslie, is one of the few actors to show any life or animation. (The others are Zooey Deschanel, who plays the inspirational music teacher Ms Edmonds and Bailee Madison who plays Jess's little sister May Belle). Josh Hutcherson, who plays Jess, rather overdoes the moody, misunderstood teenager thing, and apart from Deschanel the adults all seem a pretty gloomy bunch.Like the previous reviewer I suspect that this film will do better in America than Britain, although for different reasons to the ones he gives. (Patterson's book, after all, is frequently referred to as a "beloved American classic", whereas nobody in Britain seems to have read it or even to have heard of it before the film came out). He finds the film too religious for British tastes, but I actually found its attitude to religion ambivalent (although Patterson herself claims to be a Christian writer). A key scene comes when Jess and Leslie go to church together. Leslie, whose family are non-religious and has never been to church before, is inspired and uplifted by the Christian message, which she finds beautiful, whereas Jess regards Christianity as boring and churchgoing as an unwelcome duty imposed upon him by his devoutly religious parents.I know that this is supposed to be a deeply meaningful film with an uplifting message about facing up to loss and bereavement and the power of the imagination to overcome unhappiness, a film in which even the unsympathetic characters such as Jess's father, a stern teacher and even one of the bullies eventually reveal their human side, but I found it sombre and depressing, too unsure of its convictions to provide any spiritual uplift. Even the special effects used to create the imaginary creatures of Terabithia were below par by the standards of modern blockbusters. 5/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Richard</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>A movie to watch again and again.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>From the beginning this movie shows it means business. In a few short scenes we learn of the difficulties of a young boy named Jesse in a large family that forgets he's there except when there is work to be done, and who is at the bottom of the pecking order among his school peers. That all changes when Leslie moves in next door and happens to be in his same class at school. She too is lonely, because she is an only child and her parents are too busy with their own lives. After a rocky start their friendship grows and becomes a bulwark against the troubles they each face in the world. Leslie's rich imagination helps them create their own "Terabithia" in which they can play out the fantasies of all girls and boys, of Kings and Queens, good and evil, trolls and elves. The themes and--dare I say it?--lessons contained in this movie are multiple and deep. As with many movies based on Newbury Award books, this is not content-less fluff masquerading as a story looking for market tie-ins. Some younger children may even be overwhelmed. But this movie treats those themes with a delicacy and dignity rarely found in what passes as art these days. It is a movie to watch again and again. Although the entire cast is superb, Anna Sophia Robb stands out and is magnificent as Leslie. Her portrayal is bright and delightful, exactly fitting her character. This, along with her performance in "Because of Winn-Dixie", leads me to believe she will be a major star for many years to come--if only she can successfully make the transition from kid roles to adult roles. If there is anything to criticize in this beautiful and beautifully developed tale, it is over-reliance of the makers on computer graphics in the fantasy scenes. Obviously given the importance of fantasy to the story some CGI was crucial. But in certain scenes, especially the closing scene, it overpowered the story. But this is a minor blemish on a near perfect movie.</fullReview>

	<review>
		<date>2013-04-17</date>
		<username>twhiteson</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Bland adaption of a truly moving book *SPOILERS*</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Reading the other reviews here makes me wonder if anyone reads anymore. I'm surprised at how many of the reviews here appear to be written by persons who have never read the book upon which this movie is based.Backstory: In August 1974, 8 yr old Lisa Hill of Takoma Park, Maryland was on a beach vacation with her family when literally out of the blue a bolt of lightning struck and killed her. This unthinkable tragedy devastated both her family and her best friend, 8 yr old David Paterson, son of children's author Katherine Paterson. Haunted and inspired to make sense out of the sudden death of a little girl and her son's grief from that loss, Ms. Paterson penned what became her most famous and beloved book, "Bridge to Terabithia. Published in 1977 and garnering almost universal praise, the book has a well-earned spot as a classic of modern children's fiction.David Paterson grew-up and determined that his mother's powerfully moving story of the joy and value of friendship and the fragility and wonder of life would make a good film. He finally succeeded as producer and screen-writer when Disney released this film in 2007.However, Disney clearly didn't know how to market this movie. The result was one of the most deceptive ad campaigns in recent memory, and it appears that six years after the film's release people are still being drawn by the false premise that it's a fantasy film similar to "The Chronicles of Narnia." It's not. Instead it's a contemporary story of a friendship between two young kids. Yes, these two friends imagine a magical kingdom for themselves over which they rule, Terebithia, but that's just a small part of the story.The actual story is about young Jess Aarons (Josh Hutcherson)the only boy in a financially struggling rural family. He views himself as a bit of misfit both at home and at school. He has no real friends and feels overlooked by his parents. All he has is a passion for drawing, a crush on his music teacher (Zooey Deschanel), and an overwhelming desire to be "somebody" by becoming the fastest kid in the school. After a summer of training, he's confident that he's got that title in the bag except on the first day of school a new kid (a girl!) beats him and every other boy. She's Leslie Burke (Anna-Sophia Robb), the only child of well-to-do writers and a courageous, imaginative free-spirit unlike anyone Jess has ever met.Despite this not so promising beginning, Jess and Leslie become inseparable friends. Together, they confront and defeat both real-life bullies and imaginary giants in their magical kingdom of Terebithia. They're true soul-mates. Yet, one day a tragic accident separates them forever and Jess must come to terms with that loss.Overall, this is an OK movie with decent performances and stays fairly true to Ms. Paterson's narrative. (Thankfully, it didn't get "Disney-fied" by gutting the story of its purpose.) However, it's bland and not particularly memorable. It contemporizes the story by setting it in an affluent-looking, early 21st century suburb. As a result, the film loses a lot of the book's color which came from it being set in an impoverished 1970's rural northern Virginia community. In the book, Jess and his family are very poor: for his family's milk he literally has to milk a cow and they use a wood burning cook-stove. Plus, it's a bit hard to swallow watching 21st century kids playing imaginary games in the woods in this age of Apple, Sony, and Microsoft especially when those kids are played by young teens Hutcherson and Robb instead of the 10 yr olds that Jess and Leslie are supposed to be. Additionally, Ms. Robb is too pretty and girly-looking to be a believable Leslie and the film reduces a lot of Leslie's "sparkle" to simply having her dress as some sort of Bohemian skate-rat.The biggest issue, though, is that the movie just doesn't pack the emotional wallop of Ms. Paterson's eloquent book. Sadly, David Paterson's screen-play could not match his mother's book in creating emotional depth and feeling for its characters.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2008-12-09</date>
		<username>johnnyboyz</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>It certainly achieves what it sets out to do in regards to its target audience but whether others will see it in an equally enjoyable light is questionable.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I guess Bridge to Terabithia works on the level it does because of that nasty plot twist much later on in the piece. The revelation is actually quite welcome when you think or assume how ordinary the film might've been had it not happened and the film descended into a monotony of pleasant endings after all the other pleasantries that had followed up until that point. So on one hand, credit to the picture for not bailing out and following for through with a brave finale although on the other hand, Bridge to Terabithia is nothing spectacular but does offer a specifically young demographic the chance to watch something marginally complicated but thoroughly engaging; at least for that audience.Bridge to Terabithia is a sort of Pan's Labyrinth for a younger audience. Like the Mexican best picture in a foreign language nominee, it establishes a 'real' world in which times of desperation are very much present and it places a young, perhaps troubled because of the desperate events going on around them, hero on a farm thus allowing feelings and situations to brew. For the film's hero Jess Aarons (Hutcherson), the Spanish Civil War does not offer any particular threat but a financial crunch does. All around him there is misery and potential upset. His father Jack (Patrick) comes across as the sort of individual who could blow at any minute but retains a relatively nice, fatherly, understanding persona overall ? this is Robert Patrick doing what he did in recent efforts Walk the Line and Ladder 49 to the best of his ability.But Bridge to Terabithia takes this young hero and enables them to escape to an alternate, imaginary universe only this time with a playmate to accompany them. She is the new neighbour called Leslie Burke (Robb) and like Jess, she is suffering at school with all those issues that mar school life. So like Pan's Labyrinth, these young and quite na?ve heroes are presented with lonely and somewhat poor rural lives at home but unlike Pan's Labyrinth, the issues branch out into school complete with bullying and the inability to fit in with a crowd.The two escape to Terabithia, a world on the other side of a small creek in the surrounding woods adjacent their homes and it is here in which they find their solace; their fake utopian world away from their real dystopian one. While this is great for younger viewers, encouraging fantastical play and showing real children on screen doing things the target audience may also do; I just didn't get the connection myself. In filmic terms, it doesn't work dramatically for anyone over a certain age. The characters that inhabit the school are clichés: you have the bully; the victim; the potential love interest and that young, polite, female music teacher who's the one teacher the kids can connect with. They're archetypes that we're familiar with and don't work beyond what they set out to do and that is to act as visual indicators to the demographic what 'type' of characters are on screen.The film is really all about standing up to ones adversaries; it's about defeating ones doubts in harsh times. The film makes this apparent through whatever mini narratives the two kids set up once in Terabithia and those are to do with coming up against overwhelming odds such as trolls and multiple flying/ground monsters and winning through in the end. The film is also littered with quotes and quips to do with pulling through, the most obvious of which herald from wartime president Franklin Roosevelt to do with achieving when you put your mind to it and the film's favourite: "Keep your eyes closed, but your mind wide open". There's also no coincidence the birds in Terabithia make the same 'Dead Meat!' crow the bullies make at school; clearly distinguishing what the birds are meant to be in the fantasy and that if defeating them in another form is possible then you can attempt to do so in the real world.But again, for the target demographic: perfect. These ideas are great when aiming the film at the specific audience and it doesn't chicken out towards the end either, maintaining that loss is a part of life after all. Part of watching a film is that means of escapism ? escapism into another world or time or country in which the film is made, but is it any fun when we have to pretend even further? Bridge to Terabithia is grounded in a present day Western civilisation but in order to get something different we have to propel ourselves further into the fantasy of two young characters who are making it all up as they go along. Again, this makes things fail dramatically to an adult audience and the adventures they have in the forest act as a mere visual clue as to what the beasts 'might' be once translated into the real world.It's by no means a bad film, in fact for the target audience it's pretty spectacular. I don't know how faithful it is to the 1977 book but in terms of relation to Pan's Labyrinth it's almost a carbon copy in terms of ideas right down to the final shot hinting at some sort of new order. But that would spoil both films, something I really don't want to do. I think the perfect viewing circumstances might well be parents watching the film with their children and pointing out all the good stuff as they go along, that way the children will get verbal confirmation from the ones they love and trust most as well as a satisfying film-viewing experience.</fullReview>

	<review>
		<date>2008-11-16</date>
		<username>samusarmada</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Beautiful and moving, a tale of friendship and imagination that will appeal more to adults than to children and will surely remind them of what they once had.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Much like the main characters it presents Bridge to Terabithia is caught in the middle of being both a children's and an adult's film. It delves into the areas of fantasy, hope and friendship all against the backdrop of loneliness, under appreciation and worry. Like all good films it combines the two to produce a sweet, endearing and at times heart breaking film.The film gently introduces its main characters to us, establishing family set ups and main characters positioning throughout their school system. Unsurprisingly main character Jess Adams is a bit of a misfit, his farmyard upbringing the brunt of the bullying he faces at school. Once this is established we meet Leslie Burke, the likewise equally misunderstood female character who plays the role of the slightly kooky, imaginative girl. Like all childhood friendships their relationship develops quickly, a slight sense of tension at the start is quickly forgone thanks to some tried and tested chewing gun and a race to a tree. It's both a charming and an idyllic relationship, hints of romantic interest remain appropriately as hints-never going too far but amicably showing affection and the ever popular method of having two friends have a quarrel and then make up later in the movie is thankfully avoided. The actors themselves are strong as well. I was never really a fan of Josh Hutcherson (who plays Jess) mainly because I didn't like Zathura, but here he gives a strong performance, particularly from someone his age. Likewise AnnaSophia Robb (Leslie) is also strong. Her more positive and inquisitive character is easier to pull off than Josh's (who, it has to be said has to go through varying emotional stages) but she does do her job very well and most importantly the chemistry between the too leads is there is full force. This is perhaps the area where Bridge to Terabithia will mostly appeal to adults. Children will find the early parts tedious, anxiously waiting for Terabithia itself, but for adults the two characters relationship is one of endearing innocence. Such relationships can never happen with those past that mystical puberty ridden gateway so the sense of friendship and excitement is all the more powerful here.Something that well extends into Terabithia itself. The two create this dreamland out of their own forest as a means of escaping the bullies and school that populate their real lives. Its meaning is not merely so superfluous however; this is a world where both can foster their own gifts of imagination and creativity. Terabithia turns out not to be a means of escape but a means of creation. It is a world that adults really have no business entering or have any means of even attempting to enter. As someone who long ago crossed the bitter border that separates childhood from teenage angst I felt slightly incredulous about how they could imagine such a world based on real features; but that was the thing that really got to me about Bride to Terabithia, it's a film that I can no longer fully connect with because it is an idea that has mournfully now left me. Ultimately this made the film connect to me on a different level and one that truly had a far greater impact. It reminded me of how wonderful and escapist being a child can be. How turning ordinary events into extraordinary creations was not only the norm but also brilliantly fascinating. Again, thankfully the film keeps this sense of imagination alive; whereas in most films the parental figure at some points tells the child to grow up and live in the real world here such a feeling are never presented. Though the family are financially struggling and although the Dad still asks for Jess to help out more the intrusion of family life into this world is never pressed. What really takes Bridge to Terabithia to the next level is how the opposite occurs. The real world does not take its toll on Terabithia, Terabithia makes its mark on the real world and it is at this point that the true reasoning behind growing up, the true understanding of knowing the limitation to such fantasies make their most tragic mark.Bridge to Terabithia is a film that deals with the realities of life in ways we perhaps would not want it to deal with. Films often deal with growing up when faced with adversity, but rarely do films show this sense of childhood-this innocence in the imaginative sense. It has its share of niggles-the script is aimed and necessarily written for younger people and at times you will see the characters do things you would rather they didn't. But this is because you can see so much of yourself here in this film that it resonates with you to the point at which the somewhat repetitive dialogue ("wow" "Jess over here" "wow") becomes not only understandable but also familiar. In the end this is a film that has made me wonder why I ever even wanted to grow up in the first place but more importantly it made me look up at the trees and wonder just what could really be lurking there... a valt, a gredarg, or even a chelit perhaps? You have no idea what these are because I made these creatures up after watching Bridge to Terabithia. The film gave this particular reviewer part of his imagination back, and for that he is eternally grateful.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2007-07-29</date>
		<username>Larry Peel (Larry_L_Peel)</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Bridge to Terabithia Leads to Movie Magic</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Gabor Csupo, the Hungarian born animation wizard, brings his immense talent to the screen once again in his latest project Bridge to Terabithia, the big screen adaptation of Katherine Patterson's 1977 book. Csupo manages to do what few directors have managed in trying to adapt a classic book to film; he maintains the vision and the magic of the text. Bridge to Terabithia is a wonderful jaunt into the magic and mystery of the imagination of children, and an excellent family experience.In this Walden/Disney cooperative, Fifth grader Jesse Aaron's (Josh Hutcherson) hopes of becoming the fastest runner in his class are dashed when new girl Leslie Burke (AnnaSophia Robb) outruns everybody, including him. However, Jess and Leslie soon begin a friendship, and discover a magical kingdom in the forest, where the two of them reign together as king and queen. Both bullies and their parents, which are transformed within the magical kingdom that they create for themselves, embattle the pair.Through the artistic creativity of Weta Digital, the world of Leslie and Jess comes to life in ways that Patterson's book could not possibly have conjured. Brilliant creatures, an incredible tapestry of settings, and superb acting on the part of the young stars bring the story from the page to the screen in an immensely entertaining fashion. Csupo retained the intent that Patterson seemed to have had in her book; that friendship, not magic is the key to the tale. Csupo also makes sure to portray the fact that Jess in intimately in tune with and concerned about, his family's finances. This is a feeling that many in the films target demographic, 7-13 year-olds, can relate to in modern society.Following the tradition now firmly established by Narnia, Csupo strives for, and succeeds in, capturing the legacy, vision, and morality of the original tale. Csupo has long been involved in the behind the scenes magic of nearly two-dozen animated films and television series. Josh Hutcherson is no newcomer to the industry either with a nearly equal number of roles as his director has credits. In fact Josh is set to lead another family friendly contender this Spring with Firehouse Dog, set to open in March. AnnaSophia may be most familiar to audiences as the gum-smacking Violet Beauregarde in 2005's Charlie &amp; the Chocolate Factory. Robb too has been busy in the industry, with 7 credits now behind her and 5 more completed or in post production.Disney has finally begun returning to its roots, revitalizing the 1960's family friendly, live action film. This film may appear to be a second rate Narnia copy, but it certainly has its own merits, and is well worth taking the kids or a date to.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>I read the book and saw the original movie before this, but I have to say out of the three experiences, I found the latest attempt the least enjoyable. What I liked about the book was that Terabithia was imaginative. The movie appeared a little too focused on showing fantastic imagery, while the strength of the book was its intentional ambiguity. It was a decent book, but it makes for pretty bad cinema.Having read a review that suggested that we not judge a movie based on the trailers, I disagree. Trailers are used to entice audiences to see a movie by showing clips that give the viewer a feeling of what he or she could expect to encounter during the film. Disney's advertising focused on an area that Katherine Paterson intentionally avoided. The problem with this is that with the exception of the CGI, Disney stayed pretty close to the storyline; they just created a false impression of what a viewer could expect to see. I saw the movie expecting to see a retelling, and instead I saw Disney ruin the most valuable aspect of the story.I just think it's ludicrous to blame parents for not researching it; considering Disney's penchant for taking liberties with stories, and especially given the nature of the previews, even people who knew the original storyline seemed surprised at the ending, considering how it was advertised. I mean, I've never come out of a James Bond movie with the complaint that I felt misled by the preview. 		<fullReview>Now, there are some really sad movies out there that you've seen, but in my lifetime of watching movies I think this one made me cry the hardest, and left the greatest impact on my life. Its been several months now and I still can't get over this movie and the image of the young girl Leslie Burke(AnnaSophia Robb)who changed a the life of a boy Jesse Aarons(Josh Hutcherson)who was struggling with his life of endless, bullying, parental neglecting, unobtainable love and sibling conflict. I have to admit the trailers were really deceiving, and that's a thumbs down from me, but the trailer has nothing to do with the movie. If you're more of an action type person than this movie isn't probably for you. The themes of the are primarily friendship, imagination and dealing with loss. Its an excellent movie for families, I strongly recommend it for that. Personally, I believe this movie is better than a traditional fantasy, instead it incorporated the concept of imagination as well as adding a more meaningful end. The movie is based on a novel written by Katherine Patterson, its one of the BEST and absolute best book to movie adaptations I've ever seen if not the best. It brings the general plot line as well as keeping important details into a modern day perspective from the 1970s. The modern day setting doesn't bother me if anything it makes it more relatable. The actors were absolutely terrific, Josh Hutcherson successfully portrays the depressed and lonely Jesse Aarons while AnnaSophia Robb captures Leslie's enthusiasm for life, friends and imagination. Their contrasting personalities fit well together making their friendship a more meaningful. The special effects were amazing being done by the same special effects teams of the Lord of the Rings movies. I think the special effects were really important in terms of portraying the imaginations of Leslie and Jesse to the audience it would have been redundant to watch them just play fight monsters by whacking a tree with a stick. The book was more like that, so if anything the movie is better than the book in terms of communicating imagination to the audience. I really must emphasize that AnnaSophia Robb is absolutely beautiful she really is, she gives off this innocent but fun image and I think that is important in order to make the death of Leslie Burke more tragic because the audience can more deeply admire the character. In the original book and another adaptation that was made in the 80s. Leslie Burke is portrayed as a girl looking more like a boy which turns people off in terms of her character, even though the imagine of the character is not traditionally used, her blonde hair, quirky, fun personality gives and brings Leslie more to life. I'm not a big movie critic, I don't like to give movies really bad ratings and such because every movie is different I respect that, I just go with what I feel and if the movie is simply good or not and what impact did it have on myself. This movie is a beautiful tale of friendship it really touched me, I can really relate myself. You might be disappointed when you see this movie and its not what you expect but to quote Leslie Burke from the movie, "Close your eyes, but keep your mind wide open!" its something that I'll never forget as if Leslie were my own friend and that's how you keep her alive.</fullReview>

Death is never an end, but a new beginning. A soul does not die and although we miss the physical person, and it is hard for people on Earth to understand this, the spirits of loved ones who pass on do not leave us as they journey to the Afterlife. Whatever you call it - Heaven, the Elysian Fields, the Summerland, it is a place of eternal life, peace and harmony. I was heartened that even though Jesse's family were Christian, they did not try to convert Leslie and her family, and Jesse's father told him that Leslie's spirit would go to Heaven. Or perhaps Leslie actually went to a free Terabithia. Perhaps her parents, both writers, could write her story. Perhaps Jesse could illustrate it so others can share in Jesse's beauty and her unique magic, teaching others to find their own unique expression.It is sad to see some parents pan this movie. I have known very young children who have had loved ones die and children should be taught about death, and that it is not as final as it seems. That a soul is eternal, and even though it is sad to lose someone, their beauty that enhanced you, the world and the Universe will never be lost as we too are on our scared journeys, touching others as we travel along our chosen paths.As I walk through the forest, along the leaf carpeted path, I hear the trees whisper my name. I cross the bridge to Terabithia. Come with me. See the castle, the lake, the ocean, see the entire kingdom and the now free people ... freed from the Dark Master. See the Dark Master realise the error of his former ways and cry and beg forgiveness. See the people forgive him and eventually make him King. 		<shortDescription>Overdone? But Still Good Somehow?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Just watched this movie last night with my wife (who fell asleep halfway through).The problem I had with Bridge to Terabithia, besides the fact that I was able to predict Leslie's death after the first 30 minutes as well as other major plot twists like the bully's redemption, was that Leslie's character was FAR too smart for a child of her age, and there were too many little incidents that would never happen to young kids in America.For instance: the bus driver would NEVER kick a kid off the bus simply because another accused them of tripping them. The parents of each of the main characters were too stereotypical and the contrast between the "ultra-liberal" and "ultra-conservative" parents is a bit overplayed and oversimplified. The contrast between the homes, even down to set design and lighting, was almost too stark to be believable. Couple this with Leslie's encyclopedic vocabulary and genius IQ and you start to verge dangerously close to losing the audience's suspension of disbelief.However, all this said, Leslie's character was so endearing and the greater truths she seemed to represent about the power of dreams were enough to bring me to tears anyway when her death did (predictably) arrive. For this, I feel almost violated. I concede defeat, though. As much as I tried NOT to cry, I just couldn't restrain myself. "Thank GOD I didn't see this in the theater," I thought.The other main complain I had is that the side plot about the sister seemed to come out of nowhere at the end. Although I appreciated the comment it made about the value and importance of setting a good example and showing love to those who look up to you and want to emulate you, in hindsight it kinda seems contrived. But again, the magic of the filming somehow still managed to bring me to tears when he brought his little sister over that bridge, because it made me think of my own little sisters growing up.So here you have a movie that was in every way predictable, overbaked, and melodramatic, but still managed to wrench a very deep and sincere emotional response from a very skeptical viewer. Take that critique for what it's worth, I guess.Bridge to Terabithia a GOOD movie? Or just an average movie with some very moving themes? You decide.</fullReview>

		<date>2007-08-19</date>
		<username>Roland E. Zwick (magneteach@aol.com)</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>charming, low-keyed fantasy</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Bridge to Terabithia" is that rare movie about enchantment that is actually enchanting. Based on the novel by Katherine Paterson, this is like a more kid-friendly version of "Pan's Labyrinth," in that both films deal with unhappy children who retreat into a fully realized world of fantasy - the difference being that, in this case at least, it is a world seemingly of their own making.Jesse Aarons, a local farm boy, is a regular target of bullies at school, while Leslie Burke is the new kid in town, never an easy thing to be when you're an underclassman in a school full of obnoxious, belligerent eighth graders who don't mind showing you who's boss every time you walk down the hallway or dare to use the lavatory. The two new friends obviously need a place to get away from their troubles, so in the forest nearby, they create the imaginary kingdom of Terabithia, filled with wondrous creatures and non-threatening ogres, that becomes increasingly actualized as the story goes on."Bridge to Terabithia" succeeds by keeping it uncluttered and simple and by focusing more on the daily lives of Jesse and Leslie than on their adventures in the forest. Fantasy, the film seems to be saying, is for those special times when reality gets to be just a bit too much to handle, but that the real, everyday world is the place we all must inhabit for most of our time here on earth. The movie is all in favor of childhood imagination, but not at the expense of real world experiences and relationships.The tricky premise is brought to charming cinematic life by writers Jeff Stockwell and David Paterson, director Gabor Csupo, and the special effects experts who understand that less is often more when it comes to making the fantastical real and believable on screen. But primary credit goes to the utterly winning performances by Josh Hutcherson, Anna Sophia Robb, and six-year old Bailee Madison, who, as Jesse's adorable kid sister, reminds one of Drew Barrymore in her early "E.T." days. All three do a lovely job embodying the youthful exuberance and openhearted wonder that are so much a part of the movie's vision and theme.Parents should note that, while the movie may be too emotionally wrenching for very young children (there's an unusually stark plot development towards the end of the film), it is ideal viewing for slightly older kids and younger teens. It's great fun, too, for adults not too cynical and jaded to tap into the dream.</fullReview>
	</review>

It's typical kids make-belief, brought to life with some cgi animations, but never beyond how we all could make up imaginary worlds when we were dressing up with bedsheets as ghosts or running around in a forest imagining it to be full of the strangest creatures when we were kids ourselves.So whatever you might expect; this is not a fantasy film. Instead, it's a drama-film, and quite an ordinary one too in my opinion. Like typical drama-films, the movie is build around harmony which then gets disrupted by a twist of faith that seems all too real, but none of us would like to encounter, while other obstacles are defeated by the strength gained from these unfortunate life-altering events.During this we are being bombarded by positive messages and pushy 'eye-openers' for kids. And although it's fair enough to say that for plenty of kids these messages might sound fresh and new; for the rest of the family (since this is a family-film right?) they sound old, worn-out, and put across rather predictable. Because there is really nothing unique about the whole story; the characters, the events, their responses and the words of wisdom that tie everything together; it has all been done.If you want a movie to put on for the kids while you work around the house or something; this one might do it for them. If you want to watch in yourself too, or aren't looking for a kids movie and just want some drama with a twist of fantasy, I'd recommend something like Finding Neverland, which works out many of the same themes a lot richer and more compelling.</fullReview>

		<score>0.3</score>
		<shortDescription>A Very Wobbly Crossing</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>There's more than one bridge that is lacking in BRIDGE TO TERABITHIA. Trying to ascertain who this movie is targeted at is one that many parents won't be able to cross, as well as gaps in almost all the fantasy elements.Teasers and trailers would have most potential viewers believe it to be on-par with LORD OF THE RINGS or NARNIA. Perhaps not in production standards but at least in the story's fantasy elements. Not so. The screenplay and direction seemed to be at odds with the story, trying to infuse more childlike wonder and imagination but being stymied by coherency and some very shabby acting and dialogue.Bridge To Terabithia is based on Katherine Patterson's Newberry Award winning children's book by the same name. Given a wobbly structure by picture standards rather than anything approaching a solid children's story, the film fails not only in entertaining visuals but also in that the themes that run through Patterson's books are never given their comeuppance. We can see them more than feel them, which is a bummer considering how important some of the issues Patterson covers in her novels.Josh Hutcherson (ZATHURA) stars as Jesse Aarons, a middle school boy with adult responsibilities on his parents' farm. Living in the boondocks, Jesse is the butt of bullies and jokesters at his school. Finding comfort in his drawings, Jesse immerses himself in them, trying to escape life's cruel realities. He's a loner even amongst his family, where his sisters and parents rarely acknowledge his existence. Then one day a young girl arrives at his school and opens Jesse's mind to the unseen. AnnaSophia Robb is Leslie Burk (CHARLIE AND THE CHOCOLATE FACTORY), the daughter to fiction writers who takes a liking to Jesse. Running into the backwoods near their homes, Jesse and Leslie form a friendship based on escapism and fantasy. But the worlds begin colliding as Jesse and Leslie take their made-up world and incorporate its sense of right and wrong on the real one. The results are no big surprise. The bullying stops. Respect is achieved. Toughness grows.The largest problem with the story is that its target audience is a complete mystery. The dialogue is junior enough to be in league with films such as THE LABYRINTH but the themes are often adult, including extrapolations on the need for God and religion as well as how to deal with an emerging sexual attraction for a teacher, and the death of a student. These dichotomies were just too broad to be ...um ...bridged.The other issue is the out-and-out misconception that this is some sort of visually stunning fantasy movie. It isn't. These elements are few and often incongruous with the trailers that littered screens before its release or on parallel DVDs that beg you to rent or buy this film, too.Bridge To Terabithia isn't for young kids and it will bore and/or anger adults to the point where they'll want to banish the DVD posthaste from whence it came.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Smart</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It's possible that next to family, nothing is as satisfying in this life as good friends, with whom to share love and imagination, escapes from the daily chores of life. Such is the good fortune of Jesse (Josh Hutcherson) in the imaginative and smart Bridge to Terabithia, adapted from Katherine Paterson's Newbury winning book. Here is a film unencumbered by the demands of eye-popping computer graphics, although it has some, but centered on such universal themes as the primacy of charity and the virtues of openness to new experiences.Jesse is a fifth-grade pariah, who's tortured among his peers for geekiness, namely being a gifted illustrator. He eventually befriends new girl at school, Leslie (a terrific AnnaSophia Robb), and they create a magical forest replete with a giant troll and menacing squirrels.What distinguishes this film from similar ones such as Heavenly Creatures (decidedly darker) and The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe is the emphasis on reality as well as imagination. Although the bad kids are straight out of stereotype central casting, the reality is that things are not going well at home for Jesse, especially with a father who doesn't connect with his son, and a school, where except for a rousing music class, Jesse must suffer the slings of adolescent dunderheads who just don't understand his gifts.And, not all turns out well, as it doesn't in real life, but enough good happens to give hope that the bridge will always help adolescents move to richer lives of friendship and love.</fullReview>

I know that there had to be parents in the audience cringing when the discussion of "God", "Hell" and "Damnation" started between the two lead characters. Within the films context, the dialog about religion was very relevant to the stories development, but there was nothing in the previews that reflected philosophical discussions about religious beliefs and especially the death of one of the major characters. The acting was well done and the emotions came across effectively. I have to blame the company who made the trailer responsible for completely misleading the casual viewer. Die-hard fans of the book hoped it would stay faithful to what they read, while the rest of us were going to see the fabulous, mystical world of Terabithia. Instead what we got was having to leave the theater attempting to explain concepts of God, The Devil and Death and Dying to little kids. The subject matter was appropriate for tweens and older, although because the acting was very convincing, it brought the whole mood from really high and cheerful to a near standstill of despair. I can't fault the kids in the film for doing their roles so well, I would've just liked to be prepared. Next time I'll do a bit more research before I lay down a big chunk of money.In the end, I would like to forewarn all parents of children under ten that unless they're more emotionally mature, you might want to watch this movie first before you let them see it.</fullReview>
