		<fullReview>Bridge to Terabithia (2007): Dir: Gabor Csupo / Cast: Josh Hutcherson, Annasophia Robb, Robert Patrick, Zooey Deschanel, Bailee Madison: Heart tugging family fantasy referencing Heaven. Terabithia is a place that comes to life in the forest within the imaginations of two middle school friends played by Josh Hutcherson and Annasophia Robb. Directed by Gabor Csupo with an eye for detail as trees come to life and creatures are in pursuit. Although aimed at families, it reckons caution for young children due too scary sequences and themes regarding death. Formula structure teaching a powerful lesson about friendship yet seems to anxious to go for the tear jerker response. Hutcherson learns the biggest lesson as he escapes into a world shared with the smart and witty Robb. Robert Patrick is the stereotypical father who spends much of the film calling down his son and not realizing his son's talent. His turnabout in the end seems more plot convenient than genuine. Zooey Deschanel is wasted as a beautiful music teacher who takes interest in Hutcherson and invites him on an outing that he will soon regret. Bailee Madison plays Hutcherson's younger sister. There is definitely room for discussion for families while the film is somewhat depressing in the My Girl variety. We are asked to rethink our options and build on friendships as oppose to tearing down the bridge with regret. Score: 5 / 10</fullReview>

		<fullReview>In general, it's hard to judge a Disney movie or TV series on it's level of honest acting or realistic situations, since both are almost always atrociously abused. Whatever acting school Disney is sending these kids through, they really need to stop; immediately. One would never confuse the polished, mugging, child actors in this film with real children. The story is simply enough. Two put- upon misfits, who are picked on in school, team up in their after-school hours to create a fantasy life in the woods surrounding their homes. This make-believe world provides a refuge from the daily abuse they must endure at school and (for the boy), at home. This fantasy world is chock full of completely unrealistic CG effects, and one particularly bad scene has the two characters running in front of a laughably bad green screen effect. A tacked on sentimental ending, which appears to come from nowhere, and with an uncertain moral plummets the last 20 minutes of the film into bizarre territory. Not quite sure what the moral of the story is supposed to be. I suppose young boys should be wary of developing a crush one of their teachers, since it could lead to a completely unrelated tragedy which will haunt them for the rest of your life.The film has a bunch of story lines that are disjointed and never fully developed. However, it does get 3 stars for one scene that mildly attempts to criticize the absurdity of fundamentalist Christianity. Now, keep in mind, this is a Disney film, so it's not too controversial.Anyone who wants to watch sweet films about real children would be better off renting the Majid Majidi classics, "Children of Heaven" and "The Color of Paradise." Absolutely beautiful and, best of all, the emotion you feel at the end of both those films is completely earned.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Some years ago I saw a rather attractive trailer for this film. Unbelievable for a Disney children's film. Unheard of. Really shocking in the context of what was still a rather slow and turgid piece.Bridge to Terabitha starts slow and sets the backdrop of a poor country boy with an active imagination who gets bullied a little. His life is not easy - but it is definitely not hard or tough. His parents and elder sisters are distracted and pay him little attention. The boy is a fine artist with a rich creative streak. A new girl comes to class at his school. She is charming, bright and has an even richer imaginary life - her parents are writers. She has no problems of any kind except that she is new to school.These two form a partnership which brings the imaginary world of "Terabithia" to life. Unfortunately - Terabithia and its creatures are only really hinted at. The film does not get going. The story talks about Terabithia and that's all. There is no quest, no adventure, no challenge - all could have been possibilities in this imaginary land. But no, the film doesn't go that way. Not at all. It continues slow, slow, trying and failing to build character, reason and flow. Then there is the COMPLETELY shocking death - which absolutely comes out of nowhere. It is also a pointless death, which adds nothing whatsoever to the story or to the development of the characters present.This film either passed completely over my head, or is very poor. It is NOT about loss &amp; separation, NOT about characters who grow through a real or imaginary challenge, NOT about growing up. It is a vacuous piece which is NOT about anything.The "Bridge" to Terabithia comes right at the end of the film. My son picked out that it was really a bridge into the imagination - but that's all he picked out. It certainly isn't the point of the film, which I am still struggling with.I am amazed that Disney made this film - a film that so singularly and utterly misses it's target audience and instead treats them to the death of one of the two central characters.Watch this film only if you want to see how to completely miss opportunities. Definitely don't watch this film with children aged 4-13.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Beautifully amazing! Better than the book...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Bridge to Terabithia is a story about a kid named Jesse Aarons who loves to draw. When a new girl who is a little odd moves in, named Leslie Burke, these two kids spark up a unique friendship. Together, they create a magical world in a wood that they call Terabithia, where they battle imaginary creatures and find a way to deal with the bullies at their school.Even though the trailer of this movie depicts it as a fantasy like Chronicles of Narnia, it is completely different. The animation takes up very little of this movie. I was happy to see that, because I was worried they were going to ruin a potentially good story, but they did the movie almost perfectly. It was very faithful to the book.The casting was really good in this movie. Josh Hutcherson and AnnaSophia Robb were perfect together, and they both are very good actors. Jess's parents were really good in their roles, and so was Jess's teacher. The writing was done well in this movie. It stuck with the plot of the book and included elements not in the book that enhanced the meaning of the story. It also emphasized the message at the end of the movie which I wish the book had done more. I liked, too, how they slightly updated it, making it a modern story so kids these days could relate to it more.The only complaint I had with this movie was the animation. Even though it didn't take up very much of the movie, I wish they had made it a little more subtle. The animation wasn't very good, and the creatures they created for it were really weird.Overall, this is a must-see film. The storyline is great, the casting is wonderful, and it is very powerful. If you liked the book, you'll love the movie.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Watching BRIDGE TO TERABITHIA, I'm reminded once again why I avoid American kid's films as a whole: they're far too twee and sentimental, heavy-handed when it comes to the moralising, and most American child actors are intensely annoying. The CGI effects in this film are weird and quite poor, and for large sections I was uninterested in what was taking place on screen, although there is an unexpected and rather good plot twist that stops it from being one of the worst.</fullReview>

		<date>2007-05-07</date>
		<username>recl</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Why did this movie appeal to Disney?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In the UK 'Bridge opened at the same time as Spiderman 3. Many children were taken by their unconcerned parents to see this 12A - rated movie, only to find it sold out on its opening weekend. So...they took them in to see 'Bridge.Nearly everyone who left the theatre after its showing left distraught. Those children who perhaps should never have been taken to see Spiderman, certainly shouldn't have seen 'Bridge. I heard a few parents mutter that they would never take their children to see a Disney movie again.The first impression of this movie isn't sufficient - there is a subtext - and it has to be recognised (who is this girl who can catch sunbeams in a church?) Why though did it appeal to Walden/Disney? The novel was published in 1977, originally as a means by his mother to help her son David Paterson get over a tragedy. Thirty years later David Paterson, after completing the original draft of the script, saw his mothers work make it to the big screen.In that time though, things have changed a little in the world.It's hard not to be cynical about this - but a quick review of the movie in the current corporate environment is justified.Leslie and Jess - two children who don't exactly fit the pure stereotype of modern US children eh? One lives in a family that doesn't even own a TV (not likely to be signing-up to the Disney Channel then). The other doesn't belong to a family that's likely to contribute much to any firms profit margin. They explore woods, run everywhere...build tree houses...invent with imaginations that have no genesis in the Devils phone (with graphics). Coming back from school - they play outside - for hours and hours. No slumping in front of the TV for these two.These two kids are completely at odds with what corporate God-fearing America wants - neither are going to be likely good consumers.So...one of them is killed, in a accident outside, out in the woods...Now wouldn't that appeal to your modern corporate executive, disturbed by the concept that American children could be inclined to enjoying a traditional childhood - without (couldn't bear the thought) the constant accompaniment of the TV? Fun and games outside? What a horrible thought. Keep you children indoors, where they will be safe and sound, preferably in front of the TV.Unduly paranoid? OK, I'll accept that.But think, how did someone manage to pitch this film to the Disney executives - what in the storyline appealed to them? I don't think it was sufficient to say "Its a classic novel, it has to be filmed".I'll be willing to accept I'm wrong if Disney commission a sequel that features the mysterious reappearance of an adventurous and brave young girl - or even a re-filmed (Peter Jackson-style) alternate ending option for the (most likely lowest-selling of recent times) DVD release. Would Disney bite the bullet? I dunno. David Paterson would have reason to be annoyed. But hang on - "alternate endings" aren't so unusual. Look at The Natural (1984) a film that strangely shares the same sense of wonder with 'Bridge - and compare the downbeat Bernard Malamud novel with the distinctly cathartic movie.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2007-08-18</date>
		<username>galensaysyes</username>
		<score>0.3</score>
		<shortDescription>Narnifying = falsifying</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Before seeing this movie I hadn't read the original book; but a lot of people had. So why weren't there protests from some of them that the movie had falsified it into something it wasn't, for the sake of bigger box-office? I can imagine some junior executive at Disney with an assignment to find Narnia-like properties getting wind of a book with a magical kingdom in it, making the deal for it without reading it, and passing it up the chain, until, in time, the writer hired to do the treatment, with an injunction to make the most of the Narnia bits--castles, mythical creatures, fantasy battles--is forced to report back, after reading the book, that there _are_ no such bits: the book isn't a fantasy but a psychological study of a two kids who invent a pretend world that they _know_ is a pretend world. Disney (possibly remembering the bad image that a certain cable channel contracted when a prominent fantasist made public its rude treatment of her during the filming of her novels) is anxious to stay in good with the author of the book, so it courts her good opinion and even hires her son as a co-producer and writer, with the result that when the movie is done she publicly expresses her approval of it. Was it only money that stayed her tongue? She could hardly have missed noticing that by Narnifying her book the movie made nonsense of it. She didn't construct the story as a fantasy, and it can't be converted to one merely by declaring the make-believe world real. For example, if the children could bring it into being just by thinking it, they would have had that power from the start of the story; or, if they acquired it suddenly, it would have to come from somewhere; if it operated in only one place, the magic would be available to everybody, not only them; and so on. (I would add also that the fantasy world doesn't look like one out of two children's minds; it looks like the tricksy, ugly product of Hollywood effects technicians.) Turned into a fantasy, but without the logic of fantasy, the story no longer makes sense--unless one assumes that the kids are psychotic, and lost in their shared delusion; and then it becomes a dishonestly sentimental account of the tragedy that ensues when they don't get the treatment they need. (There's a somewhat similar lack in the book, in that it doesn't come to grips with the ethics of risk-taking, even when it's fatal.) And there's one worse thing: in addition to turning what might have been a quiet, small study of childhood friends into a semi-cartoon, Disney has also made it a vehicle for one of its manufactured teen starlets, who is most unlike a serious, honest, open, rather boylike ten-year-old. The Leslie in this movie is a posing plastic doll, without an ounce of real feeling or credibility: Lindsay Lohan + Keira Knightley + Bratz. The movie has many more problems, ranging from the script (which misses everything in the book that wasn't stated in dialogue) to the casting (most of which is off by half); suffice it to say that nothing hangs right, nothing is the right shape: this isn't the real-world book or a reasonable facsimile: it's the Janice-troll version.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Who did they make this movie for?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Here we go again. Another movie based on a popular best-selling novel whose pages have never graced my nightstand. In fact, unlike the Harry Potter series (another adventure string of books that I have no time or desire to engage in reading) I had never even heard of Bridge to Terabithia before the trailer started appearing at my local theatre. Had the commercial ads not informed me that it was based on a novel, I would have been none the wiser.Anyone that has read my rantings over years knows that I don't invest the time in the turning of printing pages. For the most part I have argued that this negligence has put me in a good position when it comes to reviewing films based on literary works. I don't have to worry about pre-conceived ideas on what characters should look like, differences between page and screen plot points and entire chapters or characters that are left out of the movie experience for any number of pacing or continuity reasons. I go in with a clean slate and I don't have to go much further than The DaVinci Code to tell you how much less frustration I seemed to devour than those who had read the book multiple times before their big screen experience.Bridge to Terabithia, based on the book by Katherine Paterson, is the story of two children, Jesse Aaron (Josh Hutcherson) and Leslie Burke (Anna Sophia Robb) who escape all the troubles and pressures of school bullies and parental ignorance by entering into an imaginative, creative world they call Terabithia. In Terabithia they have powers that are limited only in their imagination and are surrounded by fairies, trolls and some hedgehog looking creatures that snarl and attack with ferocious intent.Both Jesse and Leslie are in need of some distraction. Jesse comes from a home where his father's attention is fractured between his five children and the incoming bills are more than the incoming income. At school, Jesse is even more displaced. Bullies rule his adolescent world and his young sister hangs on him giving him little time for the things that children take for granted in modern day society.Leslie has just moved to town and become Jesse's neighbor. Her family is a little more functional, but with both parents being writers, she glides under the radar when each new book is in production. She too finds trouble in the schoolyard where the female bullies have such a stronghold on the recess population that they can even dictate who has access to the public washroom.Their escape to Terabithia was a welcome distraction.Bridge to Terabithia perplexed and challenged me in many ways. From the ads, I thought I was in store for a fantasy adventure. Instead, I was subjected to a childhood drama that felt more awkward than rewarding. As the minutes past tirelessly through the screening, I kept anticipating the trolls, the giants, the special effects that seemed so predominant in the marquee posters (me, and the hundred or so children that also looked disappointed and confused over the lack of fantasy). You really have to wait until the final scene before you are finally graced with the presence of CGI, which again would be fine if that is what you were expecting and forked out the cash for at the box office ticket counter.Further scrunching my brow was the knowledge that Bridge to Terabithia was a Disney release. This made the subject matter explored in the adventure even more head scratching. Child abuse, a conversation about the existence and punishment of God, and the death of a main character made me uncomfortable ? probably due to an understanding that 99% of the audience brought their younglings to the screening not knowing they would be subjected to such heavy handed plot topics.And then there was the few special effects in the film. The small creatures in the forest, the fairies, the troll, the attacking birds. The effects were so cheesy and unrealistic that I could not believe that the studio that the mouse built was behind the operation. It was as if they wanted to make another Chronicles of Narnia, but got Roger Corman and Terry Gilliam to helm the project.Bridge to Terabithia will be a film that might make its modest budget back in ticket revenue, but be warned, this is not a movie for the very young, and there is nothing there for the adult customer. So just who did they make this film for? www.robertsreviews.com</fullReview>

First we hear that anyone who doesn't believe in Jesus is going to hell. Leslie says she doesn't believe in Him, then she dies. Disney is like this and always has been. Steven Spielberg once commented that Disney movies used to terrify him as a child. But Disney pulls this all the time. The all time classic example is Old Yeller about the dog that saves its master from a wolf only to die from rabies as a result. Don't get me wrong. I love Pinocchio and Snow White and Dumbo, but that's where the romance ends. The problem is that Disney has become to children's entertainment what Playboy has become to the adult magazine world...outdated, unimaginative and passé. Their writers are mean-spirited, their plots shoddy and the overall quality second-rate. Bridge to Tarabithia is nothing like The Chronicles of Narnia that it pretended to be. It is a cruel movie that offers no resolution and a tawdry and ridiculous attempt at catharsis. Some will say that this is how the book was written, but then the book should have been deemed unfilmable. Thumbs down to Disney, which will probably make a fortune off this one, but Disney won't care. Mice don't have thumbs.</fullReview>

		<username>knitterhead</username>
		<score>0.1</score>
		<shortDescription>What were they thinking?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>If you've read the book, you'll know that it's a powerful, quiet, very well told tale of two rural children--one more country than the other--whose lives focus around the typical concerns of rural school-age children who grew up in the seventies.Jessie is the child of typical countrified parents, just managing to get from one week to the next. Leslie, his new next-door neighbor--is an aberration from his usual classmates. She is the child of free-thinking parents who don't have a television, and don't really conform to the social norm of their new locale.Through a series of events that loom gigantic to small children--such as races at recess--Leslie and Jessie eventually become friends. They cross a creek and in what can only pass as a modest structure (on the ground) they create a kingdom called Terabithia. It has fairies, and eventually a princess, but not body armor, sastsquatch, and many, many other cgi wonders that really have no business in this powerfully quiet story about grief and the rural setting it was written in.I find it truly disturbing that this--quite possibly the best book in children's literature, ever--was taken up by the Disney people and made into such a spectacle. I am disappointed in both Walden Media and Ms. Patterson's decision to sell it. This book was written to make sense of a grievous event. The film was made to make money, and I am sorry for those who will miss the book for the film which is nothing short of noisy, loud, and garish in its execution.</fullReview>
